        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4138fbccb6be4850924329094a7e548a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0b69ddd8-734c-4a22-b1ab-7e0b2f37c110'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhang/Xinlu">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ni/Zhiwei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zhiwei Ni</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">

R. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Dai/Yumeng">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Dong/Mutian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Mutian Dong</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d72819929" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Porous Sn (PSn) has aroused extensive attention as an advanced anode for sodium-ion batteries due to its high theoretical capacity and small volume expansion. However, as a low-melt metal, the preparation of PSn at room temperature is a difficult problem. Herein, universal chemical prelithiation/presodiation strategies are reported to rapidly synthesize PSn from commercial Sn powders at ambient temperature. The recyclable pre-metallation solvents are selected by redox potential analysis to react with Sn to form alloy precursors in a short time. Benefiting from the application of delithiation/desodiation agents with suitable proton concentrations, PSn perfectly inherits the Sn skeleton from precursors. With this method, porous Al, Pb, Bi, and Sb are successfully produced. Finally, the half-cell with PSn anode prepared by presodiation strategy achieves a great rate property (663&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and prominent cycling performance with 71% capacity retention after 7500&nbsp;cycles (502&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work may contribute to the controllable synthesis of other porous metals at room temperature.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309834-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309834-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Nowadays, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have aroused extensive attention due to their similar properties to lithium-ion batteries and large crustal abundance of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0001_R_d72819921e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Mater. Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Na has a higher standard electrode potential than Li (≈0.3&nbsp;V), which causes a lower energy density in full batteries. Furthermore, the hard carbon (HC) anode has a low capacity (300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0002_R_d72819921e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, a high-capacity anode material needs to be explored to accelerate the application of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Alloy anodes have been extensively researched as a high-capacity paradigm in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0003_R_d72819921e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, Sn is regarded as a potential anode material because of its high theoretical capacity (847&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), high volumetric density (7.28&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>), and environment-friendly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0004_R_d72819921e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Lett.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, Sn has a huge volume change (420%) in sodiation/desodiation, which leads to structural rupture, the repeated growth of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, and detachment from the current.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0002_R_d72819921e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0005_R_d72819921e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To solve these issues, some strategies have been put forward.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0006_R_d72819921e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Nano Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The design of a porous structure can effectively suppress the volume expansion of Sn during the cycle. Sieradzki et&nbsp;al. innovatively used an electrochemical dealloying method to successfully make Li-Sn alloy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0007_R_d72819921e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Porous Sn (PSn) was obtained after further removing lithium by selective solvent. However, the process involving the assembly/disassembly of batteries is complicated and the prepared PSn is mixed with carbon black. Furthermore, Zhang et&nbsp;al. fabricated PSn by chemical dealloying of Mg<sub>90</sub>Sn<sub>10</sub> ribbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0008_R_d72819921e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis of the Mg<sub>90</sub>Sn<sub>10</sub> alloy precursor needs to be carried out at a high temperature (700&nbsp;°C). Consequently, controllable and scalable synthesis of PSn is difficult due to the low melting point of Sn.</p>
         
         <p>Pre-metallation strategy, regarded as the pre-injecting of metal cations, is used primarily to address the problem of capacity loss during battery operation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0009_R_d72819921e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J. Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lee et&nbsp;al. used the chemical prelithiation method to improve the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of graphite and Si/SiO<i><sub>x</sub></i> anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0010_R_d72819921e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this process, driven by the redox potential, Li ions were transferred from the prelithiation agent to anodes. Li et&nbsp;al. employed sodium diphenyl ketone regent to obtain a high ICE (≈100%) HC anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0011_R_d72819921e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, in potassium-ion batteries, a low-cost K<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was applied as a cathode additive to improve the ICE of the P3-type K<sub>0.5</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0012_R_d72819921e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The K<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was resolved in the first cycle, releasing K ions to compensate for K loss. In fact, the application of pre-metalation strategy in other aspects is also being explored. Wu et&nbsp;al. fabricated a prelithiated Al foil via an electrochemical prelithiation method as a single-material anode, which revealed good cycling stability and high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0013_R_d72819921e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Mater. Today">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another example was the green recycling of degraded LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (LFP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0014_R_d72819921e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The LFP can rapidly regenerate by Li compensate of functional separator with lithiation additives. Therefore, the multiple possibilities of the pre-metallation strategy are worth further investigation.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we first report universal chemical prelithiation/presodiation strategies to rapidly synthesize PSn from commercial Sn powders at ambient temperature. The appropriate pre-metallation solvents are selected by redox potential analysis to react with Sn to form alloy precursors in a short time. It is demonstrated that delithiation/desodiation agents are crucial in the morphology evolution of the final sample. PSn with different morphologies are obtained by cleaning the precursors with different delithiation/desodiation solvents. This method also applies to produce porous Al, Sb, Bi, Pb, etc. Finally, the half-cell with optimized PSn anode achieves great rate property (663&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and prominent cycling performance with 90% capacity retention after 3200&nbsp;cycles (632&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 71% after 7500&nbsp;cycles (502&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Besides, the full cell with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and optimized PSn anode shows a great reversible capacity of 90.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 250&nbsp;cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work may contribute to the controllable synthesis of other porous metals at room temperature.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309834-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309834-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0001">1</a> exhibits a schematic diagram of PSn synthesis by the pre-metallation strategy. First of all, the prelithiation/presodiation agents are fabricated by mixing Li/Na metals and arene solutions. Second, Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> alloy precursors are produced by simply soaking Sn powders in the agents. In this process, Li/Na ions are transformed from prelithiation/presodiation agents to Sn powders, and the agents go back into arena solutions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0015_R_d72819921e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano-Micro Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The recycled agents can reduce production costs. Finally, the PreLi-Sn/PreNa-Sn (PSn prepared by chemical prelithiation/presodiation) are successfully prepared by using delithiation/desodiation solvents to etch the Li/Na elements from the alloys.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/81e393c4-5334-4e77-b433-760be8504fb9/adfm202309834-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/81e393c4-5334-4e77-b433-760be8504fb9/adfm202309834-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc4ce7a4-75a6-46ff-880f-51d38252de7a/adfm202309834-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/81e393c4-5334-4e77-b433-760be8504fb9/adfm202309834-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of PSn synthesis by the pre-metallation strategy.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The formations of alloy precursors have driven the redox potentials between prelithiation/presodiation agents and lithiation/sodiation potentials of Sn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0016_R_d72819921e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mapped electrostatic potential (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0002">2b</a>) shows negative electrostatic potentials of naphthalene (NP), biphenyl (BP), and dimethylbiphenyl (4,4′-DMBP) are concentrated in the middle of the aromatic rings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0017_R_d72819921e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, taking prelithiation agents as an example, the reduction ability of Li-NP, Li-BP, and Li-4,4′-DMBP agents is researched by density functional theory (DFT) calculations. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0002">2a</a>, the highest occupied molecular orbit (HOMO) levels of Li-NP, Li-BP, and Li-4,4′-DMBP are −2.468, −2.388, and −2.202&nbsp;eV, respectively. Li-4,4′-DMBP agent possesses the highest HOMO energy, indicating that it has the lowest reduction potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0016_R_d72819921e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DFT calculation results are confirmed by cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0002">2c–e</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) show lithiation/sodiation potentials of some potential alloy anode materials and common prelithiation/presodiation agents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0003_R_d72819921e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0018_R_d72819921e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Energy Lett. Joule">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Li/Na-NP, Li/Na-BP, and Li/Na-4,4′-DMBP exhibit a lower potential than Sn. In particular, Li/Na-4,4′-DMBP agents possess the lowest potentials (0.16/0.03&nbsp;V) due to the introduction of electron-donating methyl groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0016_R_d72819921e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, porous metals are researched using Li/Na-4,4′-DMBP agents in the following section unless otherwise specified.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9f517dc0-43f5-41f4-8e82-2d0f7117d5b0/adfm202309834-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9f517dc0-43f5-41f4-8e82-2d0f7117d5b0/adfm202309834-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79607df1-549d-4caa-a293-677116c83d70/adfm202309834-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9f517dc0-43f5-41f4-8e82-2d0f7117d5b0/adfm202309834-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) HOMO and LUMO energy levels of Li-NP, Li-BP, and Li −4,4′-DMBP. b) Mapped electrostatic potential of NP, BP, and DMBP. c). CV tests of Na-NP, Na-BP, and Na-4,4′-DMBP. d) Lithiation and e) sodiation potentials of alloy anodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3</a> shows the morphologies of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) indicates that blank Sn does not have a porous structure. However, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3a,b,d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), SEM and low TEM magnification images indicate that PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn have obviously isotropic porous structures, which is beneficial to promote ion transport and restrain volume expansion of Sn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0019_R_d72819921e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And PreLi-Sn possesses a more abundant channel. The HRTEM images show the lattice fringes of PreLi-Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) and PreNa-Sn (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3e</a>) are 0.29&nbsp;nm, consisting of the (200) plane. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3c,f</a> further reveals the atomic-resolution HAADF patterns of PreLi-Sn. The atomic structure is consistent with the model of Sn with a space group of I4<sub>1</sub>/amd. In addition, EDS mapping images of PreLi-Sn (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3g–j</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7,S8</a>, Supporting Information) and PreNa-Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) display the even distribution of the Sn element. Crystal and morphological characterizations demonstrate the successful synthesis of PSn. Furthermore, delithiation and desodiation solutions play a key role in the structure evolution of final porous metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0020_R_d72819921e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taking the process of forming PreLi-Sn as an example, the effect of different delithiation solvents on porous structures is discussed. After soaking Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> alloy in deionized water, the product displays a non-porous structure and the particles are glued together (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3k</a>; <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information). This can be ascribed that the violent reaction gives off a lot of heat for a short time due to the high protons concentration of water, resulting in the holes fusing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0020_R_d72819921e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10e</a> (Supporting Information) shows the product after cleaning with ethanol. The product also does not exhibit a significant porous structure. To perfectly inherit the porous structure from Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> alloy, some compound reagents with different concentrations of protons are explored. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3l–n</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b–d,f–h</a> (Supporting Information) show the delithiation products cleaned by diverse solutions composed of ethanol and DMC, PC, DEC, FEC, and DC: DEC (1:1 Vol.%) and DC: DEC (1:1 Vol.%) with 10% FEC, respectively. The ultimate products display different porous structures. And Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0003">3n</a> exhibits an isotropic PSn due to the application of a delithiation solvent with a suitable proton concentration.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/75937cc4-f1fb-48e3-883c-d41f17f4ca4e/adfm202309834-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/75937cc4-f1fb-48e3-883c-d41f17f4ca4e/adfm202309834-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1c223cb1-6e24-4b68-804b-152ec716ab2b/adfm202309834-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/75937cc4-f1fb-48e3-883c-d41f17f4ca4e/adfm202309834-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Crystal and morphological characterizations of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn. SEM images of a) PreLi-Sn and d) PreNa-Sn. b) TEM image of PreLi-Sn. c,f) HAADF-STEM images of PreLi-Sn. e) HRTEM image of PreNa-Sn. g–j) EDS mapping images of PreLi-Sn. SEM images of final PSn cleaned by k) deionized water. SEM images of final PSn cleaned by solutions composed of l) ethanol and FEC, m) ethanol and DMC, and n) ethanol and DC: DEC (1:1&nbsp;Vol.%) with 10% FEC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4a,b</a> shows the XRD of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn. Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> are prepared by prelithiation and presodiation processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4a</a>). The characteristic peaks of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn are consistent with standard Sn (PDF 89–2958). In addition, the influence of prelithiation time for Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> alloy precursor is probed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4b</a>, the peaks of Sn gradually disappear with the extent of time. And peaks of Sn vanish completely when reaction time reaches 30&nbsp;min. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) shows the morphologies of PreLi-Sn at different prelithiation times. The product exhibits a porous structure when reaction time reaches 5&nbsp;min. Furthermore, the porosity also increases with the prolonged reaction time, which is in line with the result of the XRD test. XPS spectra are tested in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4c</a>. The peaks at 487, 714, and 756&nbsp;eV correspond to Sn 3d, Sn 3p3, and Sn 3p1 states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0021_R_d72819921e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 496 and 1070&nbsp;eV are associated with Na KL1 and Na 1s states. And peaks of Sn have not been detected in Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> due to the surface of alloys covered by a complex of Li/Na in solution. Furthermore, the BET-specific surface areas (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4d,e</a>) of PreLi-Sn are ≈10.5&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. All of the tests indicate that the PSn is successfully fabricated.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0dfd8ad3-6e4c-4a93-b128-4aa08b0b84ad/adfm202309834-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0dfd8ad3-6e4c-4a93-b128-4aa08b0b84ad/adfm202309834-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88fcc233-7ad3-4bd2-8fb3-84f4a6c2179c/adfm202309834-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0dfd8ad3-6e4c-4a93-b128-4aa08b0b84ad/adfm202309834-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations. a) XRD patterns of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn. b) XRD patterns of Li<sub>x</sub>Sn<sub>y</sub> at different times. c) XPS patterns of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn. d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of PreLi-Sn. e) Pore size distribution of PreLi-Sn. XRD patterns of f) PreLi-Al, g) PreLi-Pb, h) PreLi-Bi, and PreNa-Bi, and i) PreLi-Sb and PreNa-Sb.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further verify the feasibility of preparing porous metals by chemical pre-metallation strategy, porous Al, Pb, Sb, and Bi are compounded. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0004">4f–i</a> exhibits the XRD of porous Al, Pb, Sb, and Bi made by chemical prelithiation or presodiation. The characteristic peaks of porous metals are consistent with standard cards, manifesting that porous metals are successfully synthesized. As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12–S15</a> (Supporting Information), SEM images of blank metal powder all display non-porous structures. And the porous structures are detected after etching alloy precursors. This result indicates that the synthetic strategy of porous metals is universal. This may be helpful for the preparation of other porous metals at room temperature, especially low-melting metals.</p>
         
         <p>The half cells with blank Sn/PreLi-Sn/PreNa-Sn anodes are prepared to probe the electrochemical performance in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5</a>. CV curves are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The peaks of the three anodes are similar. An irreversible cathodic peak appears at 0.12&nbsp;V in the initial cycle and vanishes in subsequent cycles due to the SEI film formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0004_R_d72819921e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Lett.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0022_R_d72819921e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, two cathodic peaks at 0.19 and 0.02&nbsp;V ascribe to the alloying reaction between Sn and Na ions. The anodic peaks at 0.22, 0.54, and 0.68&nbsp;V correspond to the dealloying process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0023_R_d72819921e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Adv.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) shows charge/discharge profiles of blank Sn, PreLi-Sn, and PreNa-Sn anodes from 5 to 250&nbsp;cycles. Compared with the blank Sn anode, PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn anodes exhibit more overlapped curves, manifesting that PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn have better cycling stability than the blank anode. It can be ascribed that the porous structure of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn relieves the volume expansion of Sn in sodiation/desodiation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ef089bf2-320f-468e-8770-496690743041/adfm202309834-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ef089bf2-320f-468e-8770-496690743041/adfm202309834-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/958f6d2f-4d4c-482d-a575-74c733044321/adfm202309834-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ef089bf2-320f-468e-8770-496690743041/adfm202309834-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical property of half-cells. a) CV tests at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charge/discharge curves from 5 to 250&nbsp;cycles. c) CV tests from 0.2 to 4&nbsp;mV&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup>. d,e) GITT tests. f) Cycling performance at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Rate property of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn anodes. h) Long cycling performance at 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Cycling performance is tested in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5f</a>. PreLi-Sn anode exhibits a low initial discharge capacity. It can be attributed to a large number of Na ions that are consumed in the side reaction and formation of the SEI layer because of the excessive porous structure. Correspondingly, the ICE of blank Sn, PreNa-Sn, and PreLi-Sn sequentially decrease due to the increased surface area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the difference in consumption of active sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0024_R_d72819921e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Nanotechnol. Energy Environ. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the reversible capacities of 393.8, 490.7, and 625.3&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are achieved after 290&nbsp;cycles for blank Sn, PreLi-Sn, and PreNa-Sn anodes, respectively. The capacity retentions are calculated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). These results manifest PreNa-Sn anode has the highest capacity and greatest cycling performance of the three anodes. SEM images of blank Sn, PreLi-Sn, and PreNa-Sn anodes after cycles are explored in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). Blank Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a,d,g</a>, Supporting Information) exhibits obvious cracks on the surface after cycles due to the volume expansion of Sn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0025_R_d72819921e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, PreLi-Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b,e,h</a>, Supporting Information) and PreNa-Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20c,f,i</a>, Supporting Information) reveal a uniform surface after cycles, manifesting that porous structures validly suppress the mitigated structural stress and volume change. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5g</a> shows the rate property of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn anodes. Not surprisingly, the property of the PreNa-Sn anode is superior to the PreLi-Sn anode at each current density. Even at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the PreNa-Sn anode still exhibits a high reversible capacity of 663&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Ultimately, the ultralong cycle performance of the PreNa-Sn anode is further verified at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5h</a>. The cell is precycled for two cycles at 200, 500, 1000, 2000, 3000, and 4000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The PreNa-Sn anode displays prominent cycling stability with 90% capacity retention after 3200&nbsp;cycles (632&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 71% after 7500&nbsp;cycles (502&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The outstanding electrochemical property of the PreNa-Sn anode is related to the design of a suitable porous structure.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To research the reason for improving the performance of the PreNa-Sn anode, the CV test, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), and electrochemical impendence spectroscopy (EIS) are carried out. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5c</a> shows that CV profiles of PreNa-Sn are researched from 0.2 to 4&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The structure of peaks does not change with the increase in scan speeds, manifesting the stability of PreNa-Sn structure at high rate cycling GITT test as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0005">5d,e</a>. The Na ions diffusion coefficients are calculated by the equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0026_R_d72819921e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Nano Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202309834-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/85719592-cdbc-4d9d-8b3e-f7bf83d7d175/adfm202309834-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202309834:adfm202309834-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202309834-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>π</mi>
                                 <mi>τ</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>m</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>V</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>M</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi>S</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                           </mfenced>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\mathrm{D}}}_{\mathrm{k}} = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}\left( {\frac{{{m}_{\mathrm{B}}{V}_{\mathrm{M}}}}{{{M}_{\mathrm{B}}S}}} \right){\left( {\frac{{{{\Delta}}{E}_{\mathrm{s}}}}{{{{\Delta}}{E}_{{\tau}}}}} \right)}^2\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>S</i> is the contact area between electrode and electrolyte, τ is constant current pulse time, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> are molar volume, molar mass, and mass loading of active material of anode. Other physical quantities are acquired in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, the PreNa-Sn anode reveals a higher capacity and average ion diffusion rate, manifesting that a sodiation/desodiation kinetics of PreNa-Sn is facilitated compared to PreLi-Sn.</div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The EIS is performed to explore the change of internal resistance during the cyclic process. Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22,S23</a> (Supporting Information) show equivalent circuits and fitted EIS curves after initial charge/discharge cycles. Notably, the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> (interfacial resistance) and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (charge transfer impedance) of PreNa-Sn are smaller than that of PreLi-Sn (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). This may be attributed to the formation of abundant SEI film of preLi-Sn due to the excessive porous structure, which slows down the Na ions' transport rate. The <i>D</i><sub>k</sub> connecting with dynamic can be calculated through EIS. The formula is shown below<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309834-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309834-bib-0027_R_d72819921e1132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202309834-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b888eb9f-7cac-4046-b8b4-4dca3dfe633f/adfm202309834-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202309834:adfm202309834-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202309834-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <mn>0.5</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mi>RT</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi>A</mi>
                                       <msup>
                                          <mi>n</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </msup>
                                       <msup>
                                          <mi>F</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </msup>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                                       <mi>σ</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {D}_{\mathrm{k}}=\ 0.5{\left(\frac{\mathrm{RT}}{A{n}^{2}{F}^{2}\mathrm{C}\sigma}\right)}^{2} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>σ corresponds to the slope of − Im(Z<sub>W</sub>) versus ω<sup>−1/2</sup>, and ω denotes angle frequency. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information), the σ values of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn are 25.5 and 11.4, indicating Na ions diffusion speed of PreNa-Sn is faster than PreLi-Sn. The result is also consistent with the GITT test. Furthermore, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26a,b</a> (Supporting Information) shows EIS tests of PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn after different cycles, respectively. Both PreLi-Sn and PreNa-Sn reveal stable EIS profiles after several cycles due to the stability of SEI film. And PreNa-Sn displays a smaller resistance in every curve, indicating the advantageous Na ions propagation kinetics.</div>
         
         <p>To verify the feasibility of the PreNa-Sn anode for SIBs, a full cell with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode and PreNa-Sn anode is assembled (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6a</a>). XRD and SEM patterns of NVP are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27,S28</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, CV curves of full cells are exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6b</a>. An irreversible anodic peak appears at 2.5&nbsp;V in the initial cycle, which can be ascribed to SEI film formation and electrolyte decomposition. In the following cycles, a good overlap of curves demonstrates the great reversibility of charge/discharge reactions. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information), the half-cell with an NVP electrode has a stable cycling property at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6c</a>, the PreNa-Sn||NVP cell displays a good reversible capacity of ≈90.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 250&nbsp;cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6d</a> displays that the full cell possesses the capacity retentions of 94.8%, 92.1%, 90.0%, 89.7%, and 89.4% after 50, 100, 150, 200, and 250&nbsp;cycles, respectively. The rate performance of the full cell is measured in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6e,f</a>. PreNa-Sn||NVP cell can provide high discharge capacity of 90.4, 88.0, 86.8, 85.2, and 83.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 100, 200, 500, 800, and 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Besides, even at 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309834-fig-0006">6g</a>), the PreNa-Sn||NVP cell still possesses a stable reversible discharge capacity of 76.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000&nbsp;cycles. And the PreNa-Sn||NVP cell can power LED bulbs. The outstanding electrochemical performance indicates that PreNa-Sn is a hopeful anode material for SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309834-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/31f5d78c-f1e2-4cb8-b4d3-598b41677f63/adfm202309834-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/31f5d78c-f1e2-4cb8-b4d3-598b41677f63/adfm202309834-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d093726-96e3-4a35-96ca-ce8eed8d9f39/adfm202309834-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/31f5d78c-f1e2-4cb8-b4d3-598b41677f63/adfm202309834-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309834-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309834" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical property of full-cells. a) Schematic diagram, b) CV tests at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup>, c) cycle performance, d) capacity retention, e) charge/discharge profiles, f) rate property, and g) long cycle performance of PreNa-Sn||NVP cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309834-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309834-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we propose universal chemical prelithiation/presodiation strategies to controllably synthesize PSn from commercial Sn powders at ambient temperature. The recyclable pre-metallation solvents are selected by redox potential analysis to react with commercial Sn to form alloy precursors. The used delithiation/desodiation solvents play a key role in the structure of the ultimate product. Along with the application of delithiation/desodiation agents with suitable proton concentrations, PSn perfectly inherits the Sn skeleton from precursors. As a verifier of the method, porous Al, Pb, Bi, and Sb are successfully produced. Finally, the half-cell with PreNa-Sn anode achieves a great rate property (663mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and prominent cycling performance with 90% capacity retention after 3200&nbsp;cycles (632&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 71% after 7500&nbsp;cycles (502&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Besides, the full cell with NVP cathode and PreNa-Sn anode shows a great reversible capacity of 90.7&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 250&nbsp;cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work may contribute to the controlled synthesis of other porous metals at room temperature.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
